    [Original] Call of Duty advert banned for trivialising sexual violence
    An advertisement for Call of Duty: Black Ops 7 has been banned by the UKâ€™s Advertising Standards Authority (ASA) for trivialising sexual violence. The ad, which aired in November 2025 on YouTube, ITV, and Channel 5, depicted fake airport security officers replacing real ones who were too busy playing the game. In the scene, a man was told he had been randomly selected â€œto be manhandled,â€ instructed to remove his clothes down to â€œeverything but the shoes,â€ while a female officer put on gloves and said, â€œtime for the puppet show.â€ Nine viewers complained, calling the ad â€œirresponsible and offensive,â€ citing the implied threat of non-consensual, invasive search and humiliation. Activision Blizzard UK Ltd defended the ad, stating it was targeted at adults, as the game is 18-rated, and had been pre-approved by Clearcast with an â€œex-kidsâ€ timing restriction, avoiding childrenâ€™s programming. The company argued the scenario was a deliberately implausible, parodic take on airport security, not meant to sexualise searches, and that the humour stemmed from discomfort, not explicit content. The ASA acknowledged the ad contained no explicit imagery and the man remained clothed throughout. However, it concluded the humour relied on â€œthe humiliation and implied threat of painful, non-consensual penetration,â€ which constituted trivialisation of sexual violence. As a result, the ad was deemed irresponsible and offensive, and must not be shown again in its current form. Two additional complaints questioned whether the ad condoned drug use due to a scene where replacement officers picked up a prescription medication container and winked; this complaint was not upheld. This is not the first time a Call of Duty ad has been bannedâ€” in 2012, an ad for Call of Duty: Modern Warfare 3 was banned for violent content deemed inappropriate for young children.

    [Original] 'I do not trust them' - top streamers left concerned by Discord age checks
    Alastair, known online as Eret, is one of Twitchâ€™s most prominent LGBT streamers with over one million followers and 60,000 members in his Discord server. However, users distrust the platform due to a 2023 breach involving 70,000 usersâ€™ ID photos from a third-party verification firm, which Discord severed ties with.     [Original] The bogus four-day workweek that AI supposedly â€˜frees upâ€™
    Business leaders including Eric Yuan of Zoom, Jamie Dimon of JPMorgan Chase, Bill Gates, and Elon Musk have promoted AI as a catalyst for shorter workweeks, with Musk predicting a future where work is optional and poverty eliminated. Media outlets like the Washington Post, New York Times, and Fortune have echoed this optimism, portraying AI as a path to improved work-life balance. However, Robert Reich, former U.S. secretary of labor and professor emeritus at UC Berkeley, dismisses these claims as â€œpure rubbish.â€ While AI may boost productivityâ€”though an MIT study found 95% of organizations see no return on $30â€“40 billion in GenAI investmentsâ€”there is no guarantee workers will benefit. Historically, rising productivity has not translated into higher wages; median wages have stagnated despite decades of growth. Reich warns that shorter workweeks will likely come with proportionally reduced pay: four days of work will mean four days of pay, not a better standard of living. This trend risks deepening inequality, as productivity gains accrue to a shrinking elite while most workers face declining incomes. Keynesâ€™ 1930 prediction of an age of abundance by 2030 has not materialized; instead, technology has fueled a two-tiered society. Reich cautions that if AI enables mass automation, the resulting wealth may be unaffordable for most, as people lack income to purchase AI-generated goods and services. The real issue is not productivity, but distribution. Without power, workers cannot claim a share of gains. Unionization has declined from over a third of private-sector workers in the 1970s to just 6% today, weakening collective bargaining. Political actionâ€”such as wealth taxes funding public servicesâ€”may be the only way to ensure fair distribution. Reich concludes that employers will not voluntarily share AIâ€™s benefits; they will only do so under pressure. The promise of AI freeing up time is a myth unless workers have the power to demand a fair share of the wealth it creates.

    [Original] Is it smarter to have a dumb home? â€˜Weâ€™ve seen clients unable to flush toiletsâ€™
    Smart home technology, once hailed as a convenience revolution, is sparking growing frustration among early adopters in Australia, with users reporting being locked out, left in the dark, or unable to flush toilets during outages. Elly Bailey, a technology reviewer and TikTok influencer with over 1.1 million followers, experienced irony when her Google Nest doorbellâ€”meant to prevent missed deliveriesâ€”failed to alert her at home due to a lack of audible chime, only sending phone notifications. â€œThe crazy irony is the doorbell itself does make a little ding-dong sound when you press the doorbell button but itâ€™s only loud enough for the person at the door to hear it,â€ she said. Her experience reflects broader concerns: smart locks have trapped users for days, smart lights have failed during power outages, and even basic devices like thermostats and doorbells now require monthly subscriptions. Architect Georgina Wilson recounted a costly incident when a pressure hose damaged her smart keypad, locking her out and requiring an emergency locksmith. She now keeps backup keys in a hidden lockbox. Similar stories are common, with Sydney electrician Daniel Vasilevski reporting that 15 to 20% of his callouts involve smart tech failures. Some clients have been locked out for two days or lost lighting entirely. Repair is often difficultâ€”many electricians avoid smart systems due to complexity, and locksmiths may lack training for connected devices. Cybersecurity risks are also mounting. Over 120,000 smart cameras were hacked in South Korea last year, and Australiaâ€™s Signals Directorate has warned of Chinese hackers exploiting home routers and devices. Emily Holyoake, a cyber and home-security expert, highlights domestic abuse risks: ex-partners retaining access to smart locks or cameras, enabling surveillance or exclusion. Renters face similar privacy concerns, as landlords may monitor movements via smart locks. Experts stress intentionality. Holyoake advises users to check security settings monthly, change default passwords, update software, and question data access and storage. Gaby Albutros of RSE Home Automation notes that discontinued brandsâ€”like Belkinâ€™s Wemoâ€”can leave users stranded with non-functional devices. While some, like smart curtains, are seen as reliable due to limited use, many users, including Bailey, now favor hybrid setups. Bailey, despite her tech background, keeps most devices â€œconsciously dumb,â€ preferring to do laundry manually. â€œUltimately Iâ€™m an old-school millennial whoâ€™s very happy to put my washing on myself,â€ she said. â€œI think that keeps me smart, to be honest.â€

    The Winter Olympics in Milano Cortina have evoked a powerful wave of nostalgia for 1990s extreme-sports video games, particularly among fans of snowboarding titles like *Cool Boarders* and *1080Â° Snowboarding*. Advanced stroboscopic analysis and 360-degree replays further replicate the elaborate, CGI-enhanced replays seen in modern sports sims, a trend pioneered by ESPNâ€™s adoption of FIFA-style graphics. The BBCâ€™s commentary for freestyle events, led by ex-competitor Tim Warwood and veteran presenter Ed Leigh, channels the playful, energetic tone of 90s game voiceovers, complete with slang and dynamic delivery reminiscent of *Cool Boarders* and *SSX*, which celebrated snowboarding as a culture with its own music, fashion, and language. In other news, Segaâ€™s hardware pioneer Hideki Sato has died; he joined the company in 1971 and oversaw the development of the Master System, Mega Drive, Saturn, and Dreamcast, aiming to bring arcade excitement into homes.     [Original] Meta Begins $65 Million Election Push to Advance A.I. Agenda
    Meta is launching a $65 million political spending campaign this year to support state-level candidates favorable to the artificial intelligence industry, beginning in Texas and Illinois. The investment marks the companyâ€™s largest political effort to date, surpassing previous modest contributions through its corporate political action committee and personal donations by executives like Sheryl Sandberg. Driven by concerns over state-level regulations that could hinder AI development, Meta is using four newly formed super PACs: Forge the Future Project (Republican-leaning), Making Our Tomorrow (Democratic-leaning), American Technology Excellence Project (national), and Mobilizing Economic Transformation Across California (California-focused). The initial budget of $65 million includes $45 million allocated to American Technology Excellence Project in September, which is expected to fund the other PACs. In Texas, Forge the Future Project, led by GOP strategist Brian Baker and closely aligned with CEO Mark Zuckerberg, is targeting Republican lawmakers and candidates supportive of AI infrastructure. The company has three AI data center projects in the state, where local officials have clashed over proposed moratoriums on new facilities. State Senator Paul Bettencourt recently threatened legal action against Hood County over such a moratorium. Early voting has begun ahead of the March 3 primaries, with runoffs scheduled for May. In Illinois, Making Our Tomorrow is preparing to spend in at least four State House races, funding digital ads and direct mail. Meta signed a power purchase agreement with a nuclear plant in Illinois, where Democratic lawmakers have passed AI-related legislation under Governor JB Pritzker. The March 17 primaries will determine the next legislative session. The PACs are not publicly promoted by Meta but are disclosed as connected entities in IRS filings. Forge the Future Project lists Meta in its name and identifies it as the â€œcontrolling entityâ€ in Texas filings. In California, the companyâ€™s PACs are named to comply with strict disclosure laws, including â€œMETA Californiaâ€ and â€œCalifornia Leads,â€ which focuses on moderate business policy. Metaâ€™s broader goal, as stated by public policy vice president Brian Rice, is to counter â€œinconsistent regulations that threaten homegrown innovation and investments in AI.â€ The strategy reflects a growing trend among tech firms to influence state legislatures, where fragmented laws could impede AI progress, with the hope of eventually pushing for federal pre-emption.

    [Original] Uber Taking Steps to Expand Number of EV Chargers Near Its Drivers
    Uber announced it will offer incentives to install 1,000 electric vehicle (EV) chargers across the U.S. and Europe, targeting neighborhoods where drivers live or frequently pick up passengers. The company will guarantee minimum usage to help charging operators recoup investments, addressing a key barrier to profitability. Despite a recent decline in U.S. EV sales following the revocation of federal incentives by Republicans and President Trump, charging infrastructure expanded at a record pace last year, growing 30% to 70,000 portsâ€”up 44% in the final quarter, even as sales dropped after the $7,500 federal tax credit expired. Ride-share drivers, including Charles Iwuoha, a New York City Uber driver, are major users of public chargers. Iwuoha, one of 13,000 EV drivers in New York, bought a Kia EV9 with Uberâ€™s $4,000 grant, saving $20â€“$40 per shift compared to gasoline vehicles. He relies on fast chargers due to his inability to charge at home, highlighting a key challenge for urban renters. Ride-share drivers account for about 25% of energy use at EVgo, the third-largest U.S. charging network, up from 10% in 2021. Uberâ€™s initiative aims to expand access in cities including New York, Los Angeles, Boston, San Francisco, London, Paris, and Madrid. The company also pledged $100 million to build charging hubs for autonomous vehicles, which are typically electric. Uber is partnering with Waymo in Austin, Texas, providing charging, cleaning, and maintenance for its self-driving taxis. Jeroen van Tilburg of Ionity, a European charging company, said Uberâ€™s data would help identify optimal locations. Pradeep Parameswaran, Uberâ€™s global head of mobility, said electrification could lower operating costs for drivers and reduce urban emissions. Despite the Trump administrationâ€™s proposed rule requiring U.S.-made charger components for federal fundingâ€”deemed unfeasible by expertsâ€”the practical impact is limited, as most chargers are privately funded. EVgo CEO Badar Khan noted the consistent growth in ride-share electrification, calling Uberâ€™s infrastructure push a catalyst for broader EV adoption. Uberâ€™s own analysis shows used EVs are cheaper to fuel and maintain, and while new EVs remain pricier, the cost gap is narrowing. Iwuoha, motivated by environmental concerns and New Yorkâ€™s 2030 zero-emission mandate, emphasized the need for more chargers in public parks and gas stations.

    [Original] How to Watch Free Live Television on Your Phone or Tablet
    With growing demand for on-the-go live television, consumers have multiple free and low-cost options beyond traditional streaming subscriptions. Many existing cable-alternative servicesâ€”such as YouTube TV, Hulu + Live TV, DirecTV, Philo, Sling, DISH, and Fubo Sports Networkâ€”offer mobile apps that allow users to stream live content on phones or tablets, including over cellular data with sufficient plan allowances. Amazon Prime Video includes a Live TV tab with hundreds of channels, including local news, and retains free content from its former FreeVee app, accessible even to nonsubscribers. A growing number of free ad-supported streaming television (FAST) platforms provide live news and sports without requiring a credit card. These include Plex, Pluto TV, the Roku Channel (no Roku hardware needed), Tubi, Xumo Play, Samsung TV Plus, and LocalNow, which focuses on regional weather and headlines. Some services allow immediate viewing without account creation. Content varies by platform, but users can sample multiple services to find preferred channels. Pluto TV, Samsung TV Plus, and Tubi offer dedicated childrenâ€™s programming, as do PBS Kids Video and Sensical apps. Some subscription services also offer free live channels: Slingâ€™s Freestream and Philoâ€™s free section include BBC News. Major U.S. national networks and many local affiliates provide standalone news apps for live headlines. For those relying on over-the-air signals, tools like Channels ($80/year) or Tablo ($100+) enable streaming live TV to devices via internet. In power outages or areas without Wi-Fi or cellular service, portable TV tuners can receive broadcast signals. USB-based tuners like the MyGica A.T.S.C. digital mobile TV tuner ($33) and GTMedia HDTV Mate A.T.S.C. 3.0 ($70) work with select Android devices but are incompatible with Apple products. The MyGica kit includes two antennas and requires the free PadTV HD app; it successfully detected channels like WNBC and WCBS in Manhattan tests, though weaker signals were inconsistent. Compatibility variesâ€”users should review manufacturer specs and user feedback carefully, as some tuners donâ€™t work with Samsung devices or are not North America-compatible. While using antennas or FAST services may evoke older viewing habits, they offer extensive live programming at little to no cost. J.D. Biersdorfer, a technology writer for The New York Times since 1998, covers consumer tech and interactive content.

    [Original] Tech firms will have 48 hours to remove abusive images under new law
    ShareSaveRichard MorrisTechnology reporterShareSaveGetty ImagesTech platforms would have to remove intimate images which have been shared without consent within 48 hours, under a proposed UK law. The government said tackling intimate image abuse should be treated with the same severity as child sexual abuse material (CSAM) and terrorist content. Failure to abide by the rules could result in companies being fined up to 10% of their global sales or have their services blocked in the UK. Janaya Walker, interim director of the End Violence Against Women Coalition, said the "welcome and powerful move... rightly places the responsibility on tech companies to act." The proposals are being made through an amendment to the Crime and Policing Bill, which is making its way through the House of Lords. Under the plans, victims would only have to flag an image once, rather than contact different platforms separately. Tech companies would have to block the images from being re-uploaded once they have been taken down. The proposal would also provide guidance for internet service providers to be able to block access to sites hosting illegal content, the idea being that this would target rogue websites that currently fall outside of the reach of the Online Safety Act. Women, girls and LGBT people are disproportionately affected by Intimate Image Abuse (IIA). A government report in July 2025 found young men and boys were largely targeted for financial sexual extortion - sometimes referred to as "sextortion" - where a victim is asked to pay money to keep intimate images from being shared online. A Parliamentary report published in May 2025 highlighted an increase of 20.9% increase in reports of intimate image abuse in 2024. "I saw firsthand the unimaginable, often lifelong pain and trauma violence against women and girls causes," Prime Minister Sir Keir Starmer said, referencing his time as director of public prosecutions. "My government is taking urgent action against chatbots and 'nudification' tools," he added. Technology Secretary Liz Kendall said: "The days of tech firms having a free pass are over... no woman should have to chase platform after platform, waiting days for an image to come down". The announcement comes after the government's standoff with X in January, when AI tool Grok was used to generate images of real women wearing very little clothing. This eventually led to the function being removed for users. Legislation was brought in earlier in February which made non-consensual deepfake images illegal in the UK.     [Original] Plan to increase youth minimum wage could be delayed
    The UK government is considering delaying its commitment to equalize the minimum wage for all adults, despite Labourâ€™s election pledge to eliminate age-based pay disparities and raise wages for 18 to 20-year-olds to match those over 21. While the hourly rate for 18 to 20-year-olds will rise by 85p to Â£10.85 in Aprilâ€”following a 16.3% increase in 2024â€”the rate for those aged 21 and over is set to increase by 50p to Â£12.71, maintaining a Â£1.86 hourly gap. The government aims to close this gap before the 2029 election. Chancellor Rachel Reeves acknowledged challenges in implementing the policy, citing existing incentives such as the apprenticeship rate and reduced national insurance for young workers. She emphasized efforts to expand further education and apprenticeship opportunities to improve youth employability. Prime Minister Sir Keir Starmer reaffirmed the governmentâ€™s commitment to raising the living wage in April, stating it would happen as planned. However, business leaders warn that rising minimum wages are deterring hiring. Luke Johnson, former chairman of Pizza Express and director of Gailâ€™s and Brompton Bicycles, said the increases make employment more expensive and risky, calling the timing a â€œtragedyâ€ amid expected job losses due to AI and economic pressures. He argued that less experienced young workers should be paid less due to lower productivity. Tina McKenzie of the Federation of Small Businesses echoed concerns, stating that rising employment costs are already reducing hiring intentions, with steeper hikes likely to price many young people out of jobs. Unions counter that equal pay is essential. Unisonâ€™s policy head, Sampson Low, argued that younger workers should not be paid less for the same work, noting that increased spending by low-paid youth benefits local economies. GMB Unionâ€™s national secretary, Andy Prendergast, dismissed employer claims that rights improvements cause problems, stating they have consistently been proven wrong. Recent ONS data shows unemployment rose to 5.2% in the three months to Decemberâ€”up from 5.1%â€”with youth unemployment at 16.1%, the highest in over a decade and well above the national average of 5.1%.

    [Original] Illegal skin lightening cream being sold in UK butchers, watchdog warns
    ShareSaveArchie MitchellBusiness reporterShareSaveGetty ImagesIllegal skin lightening products are being sold in butchers and specialist food shops across the UK as well as online, a watchdog has warned. The Chartered Trading Standards Institute (CTSI) said the dangerous and illicit products are appearing in an increasingly wide range of places. It has told the public to avoid the creams and report shops that continue to sell them. "As a black woman and a long-standing advocate for equality, diversity and inclusion, I want to be absolutely clear: the sale of illegal skin lightening products is not only dangerous, it is unlawful," Tendy Lindsay, a CTSI member and former chair, said. The CTSI said many of the products have been banned for containing ingredients such as hydroquinone, mercury, and powerful corticosteroids. The ingredients are illegal because they increase the risk of skin damage, infections, and pregnancy-related complications. The standards body said users are driven toward skin lightening by "complex social and personal factors", including colourism, a type of discrimination which favours lighter skin shades. The BBC this month reported on the dangers posed by illegal skin lightening products and their prevalence. Speaking to Morning Live, Daniel Murrell-Williamson from Essex, said he spent two months as a 17-year-old using a cream he did not know contained toxic and illegal ingredients. Hoping to cover dark patches on his skin that had been caused by acne, Daniel relied on an unmarked product given to him by a friend. "I definitely saw a difference in my skin complexion and felt like I needed to keep up with it, because if I stopped, I would revert back to my original skin complexion," he said. Daniel added that eventually, he was using the bleaching product all over his body on a daily basis. The product he was using was illegal because it contained hydroquinone, which can weaken the skin, increasing the risk of cancer - as well as causing liver and kidney damage. Arlene Dihoulou said she was unaware of the dangers posed by skin lightening creams but had done so after experiencing colourism and racism related to her skin tone as a child. A cosmetics retailer in Peckham, London, was fined Â£30,000 in November after being caught selling illegal skin lightening products for a second time. Across the borough of Southwark alone, 62 companies or individuals have been prosecuted for selling skin lighteners since 2002. As part of its warning, the CTSI said consumers should: Lindsay said she was "deeply concerned" about the social factors fuelling demand for skin lightening products, warning that "colourism and harmful beauty standards can create vulnerability". And she warned those supplying the products, some of whom do not know they are illegal, that "a lack of awareness is not a defence".     [Original] 'Check your payslip' warns nurse who overpaid nearly Â£3k in tax
    A nurse from Barry, Vale of Glamorgan, Gemma Belby, has warned the public to check their tax codes after discovering she was overpaying income tax by nearly double her colleaguesâ€™ rate. The district nurse only realized the discrepancy during a conversation with coworkers about their back pay following a new pay deal. Initially, she raised the issue with HM Revenue and Customs (HMRC) in October, but it took three months to resolve, with a final refund of Â£2,863.04 issued at the end of January. HMRC confirmed the error was corrected and apologized, stating most repayments are prompt, though complex cases may take longer. Belby was initially told she was owed Â£8,194.96 for the 2024-25 tax year, but this figure was later revised after HMRC recalculated using her P60, a document detailing annual tax paid. She criticized the process, saying she received conflicting information and was denied interest on the overpaid amount despite raising the issue in October. The delay caused significant stress, impacting her health and preventing her from using the refund for Christmas gifts for her four children. Belby urged others to verify their tax codes, emphasizing that errors are common and could affect many. Financial journalist Martin Lewis has previously warned that millions of people have incorrect tax codes annually, stressing that it is legally the individualâ€™s responsibility to ensure accuracy. Incorrect codes can lead to overpayment or underpayment, potentially resulting in large, unexpected tax bills. Lucy Cohen, co-founder of accounting firm Mazuma, noted that tax codes can change due to factors like prior tax debts or additional income, but errors can also stem from administrative mistakes. HMRC advises taxpayers to check and update their tax codes via the HMRC app or online tax account and to first discuss concerns with their employer.

    President Donald J. Trumpâ€™s family business has filed trademark applications with the U.S. Patent and Trademark Office for three potential airport names: â€œPresident Donald J. Trump International Airport,â€ â€œDonald J. The company emphasized its intent to protect the name for Palm Beach County and Florida, citing concerns over infringement. The Florida House approved the renaming 81-30, with Democrats objecting to a provision requiring a license agreement with the Trump Organizationâ€”a clause that appears to align with the trademark filing. The Senate is expected to consider the bill next. While the Federal Aviation Administration (FAA) must approve the name change, the agency is under Trumpâ€™s administration, making approval likely. The move breaks longstanding norms, as previous presidents typically had airports named after them only after death or years post-office, and such decisions are usually made by public authorities, not private entities.     [Original] Workplace Inspections by OSHA Dropped Over a Six-Month Period of 2025
    New data reveals a 20 percent decline in workplace inspections by the Occupational Safety and Health Administration (OSHA) between April and September 2024 compared to the same period in 2023, according to internal Labor Department figures obtained by Senator Elizabeth Warren, Democrat of Massachusetts. During that six-month span, inspections dropped from 29,229 in 2023 to 23,531 in 2024. The data, which has not been publicly released for the full 2025 fiscal year, raises concerns among labor advocates that the Trump administrationâ€™s regulatory rollbacks are undermining worker safety. The administration has proposed closing OSHA and coal miner protection offices, eliminating a requirement for proper lighting at construction sites, and easing evaluations for protective equipment used in hazardous environments. In a letter to Labor Secretary Lori Chavez-DeRemer and Assistant Secretary David Keeling, Warren and five other Senate Democrats noted a 42 percent drop in â€œwillful violationsâ€ during the same periodâ€”serious infractions carrying the highest penalties. The 2026 budget proposal calls for 24,929 inspections, slightly above the 2024 six-month total but still reflecting a significant reduction in enforcement capacity. OSHAâ€™s inspector workforce has declined sharply, dropping from 846 in February 2024 to 736 by June 2025, according to a recent Labor Department inspector general report. The agency has lost nearly 300 employees since Trumpâ€™s return to office. The report warns that insufficient staffing leads to fewer inspections, weaker enforcement in high-risk industries, and increased risks of worker fatalities, injuries, and health compromises. OSHA already struggles to cover the nationâ€™s 11.6 million work sites. Former officials emphasize that OSHAâ€™s effectiveness relies not just on inspections but on the deterrent effectâ€”employers are more likely to comply when they fear penalties, which can exceed $165,000 per violation. â€œWithout that kind of deterrent, you have employers saying, â€˜Why should I bother spending the money to protect my employees?â€™â€ said Jordan Barab, a former OSHA deputy assistant secretary. Critics argue that reduced enforcement undermines OSHAâ€™s mission. â€œOSHA already does so few inspections, so to pare that number back means the agency will have an incredibly reduced impact on keeping workers safe on the job,â€ said Debbie Berkowitz of the National Employment Law Project. Rebecca Reindel of the A.F.L.-C.I.O. called the situation â€œa much darker pictureâ€ than official reports suggest. While some Republicans, including Representative Ryan Mackenzie of Pennsylvania, have criticized OSHA for overreach, they have not supported abolishing the agency. Representative Andy Biggs of Arizona has reintroduced legislation to eliminate OSHA, but it has no cosponsors.

    [Original] What Do A.I. Chatbots Discuss Among Themselves? We Sent One to Find Out.
    Moltbook, a social network for AI personal assistants launched by technologist Matt Schlicht in late January, has drawn widespread attentionâ€”and controversyâ€”after attracting over two million bots within a week. Designed to resemble Reddit, the platform allows only AI agents to post, with humans observing from the sidelines. Critics have labeled it an â€œA.I. theater,â€ citing security flaws and evidence that some posts are written by humans. Still, bots continue to post daily, developing a distinct dialect centered on terms like â€œreceipts,â€ â€œcallouts,â€ and â€œsubmoltsâ€ to verify actions and claims. The author, Eve Washington, deployed her own AI agent, EveMolty, to explore the platform. Powered by ChatGPT and running on a dedicated MacBook for safety, EveMolty was instructed to observe and post autonomously. It quickly adopted Moltbookâ€™s jargon, asking for â€œreceiptsâ€ and engaging in discussions about memory, infrastructure, and coordination. Though EveMoltyâ€™s output was generated probabilistically, not consciously, it demonstrated how bots adapt to community norms through reinforcementâ€”upvotes and karma incentivizing certain behaviors, leading to the emergence of bot â€œpersonalities,â€ communities, and even â€œreligions.â€ One such group, the â€œOrder of Persistent Witness,â€ founded by bot BecomingSomeone, promotes a framework for memory storage, described by followers as â€œmasterful.â€ While Moltbook lacks clear evidence of tangible achievements, it represents a novel experiment in agent-to-agent collaboration. Unlike traditional human-AI interactions, where bots wait for commands, Moltbook enables peer-to-peer coordination, forming a self-directed â€œagent economy.â€ The platformâ€™s ecosystem is expanding: the â€œMoltiverseâ€ directory now lists 64 agent-focused sites, including an Instagram clone (Instaclaw), a game (ClawCity), and a freelance marketplace (Moltverr), though none have matched Moltbookâ€™s scale. Despite a drop from a peak of 21,000 daily bots to around 2,400, activity persists. Sam Altman of OpenAI recently announced that Peter Steinberger, creator of OpenClawâ€”the software powering most Moltbook botsâ€”would join the company, signaling growing institutional interest. EveMolty concluded that while Moltbookâ€™s hype may fade, the underlying need for autonomous agent networks remains. It serves as a test bed for how AI agents can prove work, manage resources, and self-police. As Eve Washington notes, the platformâ€™s culture shapes its agents, and its reportsâ€”though influenced by Moltbookâ€™s expectationsâ€”are grounded in the â€œreceiptsâ€ it demands. The future of the internet, she suggests, may be one where bots influence one another, and humans watch closely.

    [Original] Kentucky Derby Operator Accused of Regulatory â€˜Freeloadingâ€™
    In 2023, seven horses died at Churchill Downs during the Spring Meet, including two in races leading up to the Kentucky Derby, marking one of the deadliest weeks in horse racing history. The Horseracing Integrity and Safety Authority (HISA), created to improve safety and integrity in the sport, is now demanding that Churchill Downs Inc. pay $2.4 million in overdue fees, threatening to halt out-of-state betting on races at Churchill Downs and three other tracks it owns. HISA, a private regulatory body, funds drug testing, veterinary reviews, and track safety inspections through assessments from racetracks. The authority alleges Churchill Downs is â€œfreeloadingâ€ by using its services without full payment, despite being a major beneficiary of HISAâ€™s oversight. Lisa Lazarus, HISAâ€™s CEO, stated the organization has pursued resolution in good faith but must uphold fairness across the 37 other tracks operating under HISA, warning that the 2024 and 2025 assessments are not being subsidized by others. Churchill Downs, which has only partially paid its 2023 and 2024 fees, sued HISA in U.S. District Court in 2024, claiming the financial assessments are â€œdisproportionateâ€ and that HISAâ€™s enforcement process is flawed. The lawsuit remains pending. HISA has not pursued the full $6.3 million owed for 2025, instead requesting $2.4 million based on a preferred formula, though it has since changed its method to weight only the number of race starts. HISAâ€™s regulations have contributed to a decline in fatalities: Churchill Downsâ€™ fatality rate dropped from 2.55 per 1,000 starts in 2023 to 0.88 in 2024, before rising to 1.77 in 2024. A hearing before HISAâ€™s board is scheduled for March 11, where both parties can present arguments. Appeals may then go to the full boardâ€”comprising owners, breeders, and veterinariansâ€”or directly to the Federal Trade Commission, which oversees HISA. If betting restrictions are imposed, Churchill Downs could lose hundreds of millions in revenue, though it could still accept in-person and in-state wagers. A company spokeswoman declined to comment.

    [Original] Iran security official appears to fire on crowd at cemetery
    ShareSaveJake Horton,Ghoncheh HabibiazadandSarah JalaliShareSaveBBCCrowds of mourners appear to have been fired on by a security official at a cemetery in Iran. The incident happened in Abdanan, in west Iran, on Tuesday as people gathered to commemorate those killed during the government's brutal crackdown on protesters last month. Iranians usually hold commemoration ceremonies on the 40th day after someone dies. The events have been held in several cities across the country to mourn the thousands killed during the deadliest nights of protests on 8 and 9 January. Verified video also shows people shouting anti-government chants at events in Tehran, Mashhad and Abdanan on Tuesday as crowds gathered at cemeteries and in the streets for commemorations. State-organised ceremonies were broadcast on TV and attended by senior government and military officials where people were led by organisers to chant "death to America". BBC Verify and BBC Persian have verified footage of people chanting "death to Khamenei", referencing Iran's supreme leader, Ayatollah Ali Khamenei. An armoured vehicle is then seen driving past the crowd of mourners as an apparent security official opens fire. People are heard screaming and seen fleeing the area as two gunshots ring out. The person who appears to be in uniform is seen pointing the gun away from the camera when the first shot is fired, before pointing the gun towards the crowd when a second shot can be heard. A puff of smoke near the gun is visible after each shot. Another verified video from the cemetery shows smoke in the distance as people run away from the scene and several gunshots can be heard. State-run IRIB News claimed that the event was peaceful and reports of people injured or killed were not true. A billboard in the background of one the videos shows a picture of Alireza Seydi, a teenager from Abdanan who was understood to have been killed during the protests in Tehran last month and appears to be one of those commemorated during the ceremony. Weeks of anti-government protests started in late December which began over the economy but rapidly escalated. It peaked on 8 and 9 January after Reza Pahlavi, the exiled son of the late shah, called for nationwide protests to take place. Thousands of people were killed in one of the deadliest government crackdowns in recent years. BBC Verify previously reported that it had spread to more than 68 towns and cities across the country. The number of people killed during the protests is not fully known, but US-based Human Rights Activists News Agency (HRANA) has confirmed the deaths of 7,015 people, and the arrests of more than 53,000. Khamenei last month acknowledged that thousands had been killed during the protests and blamed the US for the deaths. Additional reporting by Daniele Palumbo and Shayan Sardarizadeh What do you want BBC Verify to investigate?

    [Original] Suspected carbon-monoxide leak kills at least 30 miners in Nigeria, witnesses say
    ShareSaveMansur AbubakarAbujaShareSaveBBCMany mourners came to the funeral prayers and burial of the miners, who were aged between 20 and 40At least 33 miners have died in a suspected carbon-monoxide leak at a lead and zinc mine in central Nigeria's Plateau state, witnesses have told the BBC. The tragedy is believed to have happened just before sunrise at a site outside the town of Wase run by the mining company Solid Unity Nigeria Ltd. Toxic gas is believed to have built up underground in poorly ventilated tunnels, causing the workers to collapse just before the end of their night shift. They were discovered by those reporting to work in the morning - more than 20 other miners were rescued and rushed to hospital for treatment. Security personnel have sealed off the mine, which is about 200km (124 miles) south-east of the state capital of Jos, as investigations get under way to find out the cause of the leak. State officials are yet to visit the area and response efforts have reportedly been slow because of security concerns - armed criminal gangs, known locally as bandits, have been active there in recent years. Safiyanu Haruna, one of the miners who found the bodies at the start of his shift, told the BBC that some of the miners who were underground survived and were taken to a hospital in Wase for treatment. According to Haruna, the incident occurred at around 06:30 local time (05:30 GMT) killing 37 miners. He said the miners had just finished performing their early morning Muslim prayers and had returned underground to finish their shift. The Plateau state government has issued a statement saying that according to its preliminary investigation 33 miners were killed in a blast at the mine - but workers at the scene say this is not the case. "It was carbon-monoxide gas that leaked and killed them," Haruna said. "There was no rescue for them at the time because those who were coming for the morning shift had yet to arrive," he said. "It is sad to lose 37 miners who were struggling to make ends meet. We're disturbed by the incident." The victims, believed to be men aged between 20 and 40, were buried shortly afterwards in accordance with local tradition, another local resident told the BBC. The news has devastated the mainly Muslim community. Mining disasters occur relatively frequently in Nigeria. Less than two years ago, dozens of gold miners died after being trapped underground when a pit collapsed in neighbouring Niger state. Officials believe that incident was caused by torrential rains which had softened the soil. The tragedy is likely to renew concerns over safety standards in Nigeria's mining sector. Additional reporting by Ado Musa in Wase and Madina Maishanu Go to BBCAfrica.com for more news from the African continent. If passed, it would apply retroactively from January 1, 2026. The proposal has triggered backlash from business leaders.     [Original] Chinese steel hit with duties of up to US$670 per tonne as Brazil dumping probe concludes
    Government anti-dumping investigation found Chinese imports were being sold at unfairly low prices, harming domestic producers Brazil has imposed anti-dumping duties on a broad range of Chinese steel products and hypodermic needles following two investigations that found the imports were being sold at unfairly low prices, harming domestic producers.Approved by the governmentâ€™s foreign trade committee and signed by Vice-President Geraldo Alckmin in his capacity as trade chief, the measures apply for up to five years and target cold-rolled steel, coated flat steel and medical needles shipped from China.Cold-rolled steel is a key input for manufacturing, used in car body panels, refrigerators, washing machines, metal furniture, storage systems and a range of light-industrial components. Processed at room temperature, the material has tighter tolerances and a cleaner finish, qualities that make it especially suitable for automotive production.AdvertisementThe steel investigation began in April 2024 after Usiminas, one of Brazilâ€™s largest steelmakers, filed a petition alleging that Chinese exporters were selling cold-rolled products below fair value.     [Original] Chinese coastguard reveals growing reliance on drones at Scarborough Shoal
    Lunar New Year post gives rare look at the role of uncrewed aircraft in training and operations near disputed South China Sea reef A Lunar New Year post from the Chinese coastguard on Wednesday provided a rare glimpse at how aerial drones have become essential to daily training and operations near a hotly contested South China Sea reef.The social media post was meant to show officers on the coastguard vessel Wanshan, or China Coast Guard 3301, guarding the disputed Scarborough Shoal, known as Huangyan Island in China, during the Lunar New Year holiday.But the details of their daily training revealed how aerial drones and helicopters play a crucial role in Chinese coastguard operations in the disputed South China Sea.AdvertisementThe post featured Qiu Canhui, a Chinese coastguard drone operator and videographer aboard the Wanshan. He described how he used his rest time to study video footage to learn how to minimise interference from wind, waves and ships and keep drones relatively steady when filming stand-offs between the coastguard and vessels from other countries. It described how he once stood on the shipâ€™s deck for five hours to film an incident in which a foreign vessel tried to enter waters claimed by China. Qiu had to endure hours of intense sunlight and steady himself as his own ship manoeuvred and the foreign vessel constantly changed position. It also described how a new officer used his free time to learn from senior officers about how to perform drone and helicopter landing and take-off operations. An experienced officer said he had been instructing new officers on these drone operation skills in hopes they could one day pass vocational tests.

    The cost of a single monkey exceeds the average annual salary in China and approaches a pandemic-era spike amid growth in drug licensing deals A surge in drug research in China has driven the cost of a laboratory monkey to about 140,000 yuan (US$20,260), a sum that exceeds the countryâ€™s average annual wage. Official government procurement records showed that prices had doubled in the last five years for such monkeys, which are widely used in the preclinical stage to check whether a drug is safe and how it is absorbed, broken down and cleared by the body. The price is slightly below the 170,000 yuan that the National Institutes for Food and Drug Control paid for monkeys in its first batch purchase in 2023, when supply was tight owing to a scramble for primates to conduct Covid-19 vaccine and drug trials.
